 AUTOETHNOGRAPHY
 Researchers explore a particular experience themselves and
 ­self-document this ­using field notes, audio recordings, videos, and photographs;
  also called ­self-ethnography/documentation.



               “Real” (i.e., rather academic) autoethnographic                                                   to conduct interviews more easily and
               research might involve researchers immersing                                                      ­comprehensively when they already have a rough
               themselves for months within an ­organization.                                                     understanding of the subject matter.
               In service design, we often use a short ­version
               of this: team members explore a particular                                                        Autoethnographic research can be overt
               ­experience themselves in the real situational                                                    or ­covert. When you do overt a­ utoethnography,
                context, mostly as customers or as employees.01                                                  ­people around you know that you are a ­researcher,
                                                                                                                  while a covert approach means they do not
               Autoethnography is often one of the first ­research                                                know. When r­ esearchers are ­visible to the ­people
               methods undertaken as it helps ­researchers                                                        around them, it is ­important to be aware of a
               to interpret behaviors they will see when they                                                     potential “­observer effect” – the ­influence
               observe participants. Also, it helps ­researchers                                                  researchers have on their ­environment and on
                                                                                                                  the research ­participant’s behavior simply
               01	   For a more comprehensive introduction to how autoethnography can be used as a qualitative    by being present.
                     research method see, for example, Adams, T. E., Holman Jones, S., & Ellis, C. (2015).
                     ­Autoethnography: Understanding Qualitative Research. Oxford University Press.




               Duration	                                                   Preparation: 0.5 hours–2 weeks

 SELF-ETHNOGRAPHIC APPROACH                                                                                                                          01
                                                                                                                                                     14


 ONLINE
 ETHNOGRAPHY
 An approach to investigate how people interact with one another in
 ­online ­communities, also known as virtual or cyber ethnography.



               Often online ethnographies include a                                                  —— Participant online ethnography,

 CONTEXTUAL
 INTERVIEW
 Interviews conducted with customers, employees,
 or any other relevant stakeholders in a situational context relevant
 to the research question; also known as contextual inquiry.


                Contextual interviews can be done, for ­example,       about a specific experience that they’ve had
                with employees at their workplace or with              (e.g., the last time they used the ­service) and to
                customers during a specific moment of their            ­demonstrate details of this concrete ­experience.
                customer experience. Contextual interviews                 It is ­often easier for ­people to ­articulate pains
                are used to understand a certain group of ­people      and gains when they refer to concrete examples
                better: to understand their needs, emotions,           than when describing an experience in more
                expectations, and environment (useful for               ­general terms. Contextual interviews can be
                ­personas), but also to reveal formal and i­ nformal     ­conducted rather openly, following one leading
                 networks and hidden agendas of ­specific                 research q   ­ uestion, or in a semi-structured way,
                 actors (useful for system maps). Besides, such           ­following interview and observation guidelines
                 ­interviews help to understand ­particular                (see ­participant observation).01
                  experiences as interviewees can demonstrate
                  ­actions in detail and in context (useful for
                   ­journey maps). Try to ask your ­interviewees       01	   See, for example, Beyer, H., & Holtzblatt, K. (1997).
                                                                             Contextual Design: Defining Customer-Centered Systems. Elsevier.




                Duration	                       Preparation: 0.5 hours–8 weeks
                	                               (depending on accessibility and legal regulations)

                	Activity: 0.5 hours–4 weeks
                  (depending on number and availability of interviewees and researchers)

                                                	(depending on amount of data)

 INTERVIEW
 A qualitative research technique of
 conducting intensive individual interviews.



                Researchers might conduct several ­in-depth            should be structured in a “funnel” ­manner,
                ­interviews with relevant stakeholders (e.g., front-   ­starting with general and broad ­questions
                 and backstage employees, ­customers, suppliers,        to get the participant ­comfortable with the
                 etc.) or external experts to understand ­different     ­interview and talking and to build ­rapport,
                 perspectives on a specific subject matter.              then ­gently ­becoming more ­specific and

 Researchers collect data by observing behavior
 without actively interacting with the participants.

                                                                                                                                                                      01


 MOBILE
 ETHNOGRAPHY
 Aggregated multiple self-ethnographies, taking place in a guided research
 setting where data is collected with mobile devices such as smartphones.



               A mobile ethnography project might include                                                           Mobile ethnography mostly focuses on
               10, 100, or even 1,000 participants d­ ocumenting                                                    ­customers or employees who document their
               their experiences with a brand, product, service,                                                     own ­daily routines, or follow a specific r­ esearch
               event, or similar. Participants are included                                                          task to document whatever might be of ­interest
               as active researchers self-documenting their                                                          ­regarding a given research ­question or ­topic.
               own experiences as a kind of diary study on                                                            Dedicated apps for mobile ­ethnography ­allow
               their own phones. Participants document                                                                participants to ­self-document ­almost any
               their ­experiences, but researchers can review,                                                        ­experience along their entire c­ ustomer ­journey
               ­synthesize, and analyze the collected data.                                                            and across all on- and offline ­channels.
                In some cases, researchers can get in touch with                                                       Besides text, photos, ­videos, and ­quantitative
                participants through push notifications for                                                            ­evaluations, these apps also collect ­information
                ­ongoing guidance, tasks, or to ask for more                                                            on time and location that can be used to
                 details on reported experiences.01                                                                     visualize data as journey maps or even as
                                                                                                                        ­geographic maps. Mobile ­ethnography
               01	   For a comparison of mobile ethnography with other ethnographic approaches,
                     see ­Segelström, F., & Holmlid, S. (2012). “One Case, Three Ethnographic Styles: Exploring          ­follows a ­self-structured approach, so that
                     Different ­Ethnographic Approaches to the Same Broad Brief,” Ethnographic Praxis in Industry
                     ­Conference ­Proceedings 2012(1), 48-62. For more examples of applied mobile ethnography             ­participants are invited to document anything
                      in tourism, see ­Stickdorn, M., & Frischhut, B., eds. (2012). Service Design and Tourism:

             01	   A critique session, or simply “crit session,” is often used in design and art schools, where the
                   term describes a session in which either peers or faculty critically evaluate a student’s work.
                   In service design, crit sessions refer to inviting people who are not familiar with your project
                   to critically reflect on your work. This often includes asking the really stupid questions no
                   one within the design team dares to ask anymore – similar to the approach of “rubber duck
                   debugging” in software development. See, for example, Hunt, A., & Thomas, D. (2000).
                   The Pragmatic Programmer: From Journeyman to Master. Addison-Wesley Professional.

 person as an archetype exemplifying a group of people,

                  ­patterns, or demographic and g­ eographical                                                    their background stories, including their

 DEVELOPING

 and actionable format for communication within
 and across project teams.

06
IDEATION
Ideas are usually seen as the
cornerstone of ­creativity and countless methods exist,
often under multiple names, to create,
filter and select them. Here, we introduce some
favorites, structured into categories.


     	    1	 Pre-ideation: Slicing the elephant and splitting the ideation challenge
     		
      Extra: AESEO – The ergonomics of need
     	    2	 Pre-ideation: Ideas from future-state journey mapping
     		
      Extra: Journey ideation with dramatic arcs
     	    3	 Pre-ideation: Ideas from future-state system mapping
     	    4	 Pre-ideation: “How might we …?” questions from insights and user stories
     	    5	 Generating many ideas: Brainstorming
     	    6	 Generating many ideas: Brainwriting
     	    7	 Generating many ideas: 10 plus 10
     	    8	 Adding depth and diversity: Bodystorming
     	    9	 Adding depth and diversity: Using cards and checklists
     	   10	 Adding depth and diversity: Ideation based on analogies and association
     	   11	 Understanding, clustering, and ranking options: Octopus clustering
     	   12	 Understanding, clustering, and ranking options: Benny Hill sorting (“Thirty-Five”)
     	   13	 Understanding, clustering, and ranking options: Idea portfolio
     	   14	 Understanding, clustering, and ranking options: Decision matrix
     	   15	 Reducing options: Quick voting methods
     	   16	 Reducing options: Physical commitment

                                                                       A   	Subtext chains, a physicalization of

“HOW MIGHT WE …?”                                                                 IDEATION
TRIGGER QUESTIONS




 PRE-IDEATION                                                                                                                                                83
                                                                                                                                                             01


 “HOW MIGHT WE …?”
 ­TRIGGER QUESTIONS FROM
  ­INSIGHTS AND USER STORIES
 An excellent, systematic method to prepare for ideation which        01



 bases ideas firmly on research and existing knowledge.


                Developing trigger questions from insights                                                    to take a step back from ideas and return to
                and user stories is a good way to convert                                                     the needs and ­opportunities behind them.
                ­research into a wide range of actionable ideas.
                 Use this method when you have good research                                                  This method has several phases. First, you take
                 or e­ xperience to build on, or when you need                                                key insights, JBTD insights, or user s­ tories,
                                                                                                              developed from research data (see #TiSDD 5.3,
                01	   This version of the insights/trigger questions exercise is based on IDEO’s 2009         Methods of data ­visualization, synthesis,
                      ­Human ­Centered Design toolkit (http://www.designkit.org/resources/1), as evolved by
                       ­Minds&Makers and others. All errors are ours.                                         and analysis) and use them to generate trigger


                Duration	It is common to spread these activities across several days
                          and spend hours or a whole day on each phase. In a sprint, a shallow
                          version can be done more quickly. A break is useful before the third
                          phase (invite outside experts, and ask them to prepare first ideas at
                          home without the influence of groupthink).

                Physical requirements	You will need key insights, JBTD (jobs to be done) ­insights,
                                        or user stories. Keep raw data nearby in case it is needed.
                                        In each phase you will need enough space to present the data or
                                       ­results of the previous phase, as well as pens and paper.

                Energy level	Phase 1: low to moderate
                              Phase 2: low to moderate
                              Phase 3: moderate to high

                Facilitators	                                                  1 or more

                Participants	The different phases need different groups of people,
                              ­although some people should take part in all phases.

                                                                               First phase (trigger questions): up to 15 people, preferably ones
                                                                               who are familiar with the research data or service context.

                                                                               Second phase (prioritization): 10 people or fewer who know the
                                                                               goals and strategy of your organization.

                                                                               Third phase (ideation): up to 20 people – a mix of people from
                                                                               the previous phases and outsiders or experts who understand the
                                                                               fields suggested by the questions.

                Expected output	After you’ve gone through the three phases, you will have many
                                 ideas firmly rooted in your research.

 OCTOPUS
 CLUSTERING
 A very quick group method to sort and cluster
 ideas in preparation for a decision. Everyone takes part,
 so everyone gets to know the range of ideas.


                A crowd of people stand in front of a wall         gives everyone an excellent ­overview of what
                of sticky notes. The people are arranged in        the ­material is, and ­encourages shared
                rows. The front row actively sorts the notes;      ­ownership of ideas ­between group ­members.
                the rows behind them have various support            The new ­clusters might help the group
                or p
                   ­ reparation functions. Every few seconds,       ­understand the ­overarching structure of the
                the rows cycle so a new group of people come       material, or ­suggest different directions
                to the front and every row gets a new role.          for the next step.
                After a few cycles, the sticky notes are sorted
                and the group know the content.                    Though the description here seems ­complex,
                                                                   in practice the exercise is very simple and great
                Octopus clustering will quickly transform          fun. The following guide takes you through how
                a random cloud of sticky notes into a ­series of   to set it up, step by step. After you have done
                clusters. Use it to sort large numbers of ideas,   it once or twice, most of this will seem obvious.
                insights, “How might we ...?” ­questions, data –
                ­anything which can be expressed as a few words
                 or a picture on a sticky note. The ­method




                Duration	Preparation: a few minutes to prepare the wall
                          Activity: 5–15 minutes, depending on the number of notes

                Physical requirements	You need a wall covered in a “cloud” of sticky notes, 2–3 ­meters
                                       (6–9 feet) wide. This will probably be the ­output of a highly
                                       ­divergent method like brainwriting. Make sure that all the sticky
                                        notes are hanging between belly and head height for an average
                                        participant. Clearly mark the limits of the cloud ­using tape or a few
                                        sticky notes in another color. You also need enough space in front
                                        of the wall for everyone to stand in rows.

                Energy level	High

                Facilitators	1

                Participants	                  6–30 people

                Expected output	Sorted clusters of sticky notes, familiarity with the content of
                                 the notes, and growing sense of shared ownership

 BENNY HILL SORTING
 (“THIRTY FIVE”)
 A fast, energetic way to quickly choose the most interesting
 or popular options from a large group of possibilities. This is a more
 energetic interpretation of the game “Thirty Five” by Thiagi.                                       01




                This tool will take a large number of                                   As well as producing a ranking of the items,
                items (one per person) and quickly rank them                            this exercise also thoroughly mixes them,
                ­according to whatever criteria you decide.                             starts to establish co-ownership, and leaves the
                 Use it after an ideation or pitching session to                        idea papers looking tired and used – which can
                 select the ideas or pitches which the group                            be helpful if the group have trouble letting go
                 find most interesting, or use it at the start of a                     of their ideas.
                 session to agree on priorities for the session,
                 rules of ­cooperation, and so on.
                                                                                        Step-by-step guide
                Everyone stands in a group holding a piece
                of paper. They move through the group,                                   1  	Ask each of the participants to ­prepare their
                ­exchanging papers randomly and repeatedly.                                  pitch, sketch, idea, insight, or ­whatever
                 Then, in pairs, they compare the two papers                                 on a piece of paper. It is vital that someone
                 they’re holding and assign points to each.                                  should be able to look at the paper and
                 The exercise repeats several times and the                                  ­understand the idea in about 15 seconds –
                 ­results for each paper are summed.                                          the ­papers must “speak for themselves.”

                01	   For many more, see http://www.thiagi.com.




                Duration	Participants will need to prepare their pieces of paper,
                          ­unless they ­already have them in a suitable form. This might take
                           ac­ ouple of m
                                        ­ inutes, depending on the complexity of the ideas.
                           The game itself needs about 10 to 15 minutes, plus some time to
                           check that the “winning” papers are useful and diverse.

                Physical requirements	You need enough space for everyone to move about ­safely,
                                       but not so much space that the crowd spreads out; each ­person
                                       needs a pen and one piece of paper which has one sketch,
                                       idea, or insight on it.

                Energy level	                                     Very high

                Facilitators	                                     1, perhaps more for very large groups

                Participants	                                     12–300 people

                Expected output	                                  Ranking of all the ideas, insights, or other content

                  ­portfolio or graph. Because two variables

                   needs and appeals to analytical mindsets. It is a       customer experience” against “feasibility”

                   informed decision, and even allows a strategic          too (see the “Method notes”).
                   view of the options.

 THEATRICAL METHODS –
 AN INTRODUCTION
 Theater offers many methods which can be used
 to investigate, ideate, prototype, and roll out both physical and
 digital services. They are powerful tools to investigate
 emotion, timing, tone of voice, and practicalities of the use of space.




                                                                                                            A   	Theater offers ­perhaps
                                                                                                                  the ­ultimate ­toolkit to model,
                                                                                                                  ­prototype, and play around
  A                                                                                                                with human–human or
                                                                                                                   human–digital interactions.

               often human-to-human exchanges, like in               and deliver experiences. With thousands

               But even in many digital or machine-based             the ­performing arts01 – enjoys a uniquely
               services, the technological platform ­essentially     ­mature, highly creative, and utterly ­practical
               mimics the functions of a human being as               toolset which is fast, effective, and fun.
               it processes your order, connects you, gives you       Unlike almost every other prototyping ­method,
               information, or sells you a ticket.                   the tools of show business focus on emotion,
                                                                     the core of a great experience. And once you
               Theater offers perhaps the ultimate toolkit           have gotten past the initial ­inhibitions of
               to model, prototype, and play around with             your team, they are easy to use as everyone
               ­human–human or human–digital ­interactions.           ­understands the terminology. There is no

 ­REHEARSAL
 Investigative rehearsal is a theatrical method

 processes through iterative rehearsal sessions.

                in ­service design. Unfortunately, most ­people                                               ­constructive, full-body way to examine
                ­misunderstand the word, and think it means                                                    ­interactions and develop new strategies. It is a

                service ­simulation, and role-play.

         RULES OF REHEARSAL

 SUBTEXT
 Subtext is a theatrical method that can
 reveal deeper motivations and needs by focusing
 on unspoken thoughts in a rehearsal session.

  CARDBOARD
 ­PROTOTYPING
 Cardboard prototyping refers to prototyping
 3D mock-ups of almost any physical object or environment
 out of cheap paper and cardboard.

            Method notes

             → 	
                Speak out loud: Encourage users to
                think out loud while they go through
                the given tasks.

             → 	
                Silent operators: The operators are
                usually silent. Ask them to refrain from
                ­explaining how the prototype should
                work. The rule of thumb is: if the device
                or computer would not say/print/bleep it,
                the operators should not either.01

            01	   Of course, you can always temporarily lift that rule to have the operators help the user.
                  You can consciously decide to enable a team discussion if this becomes necessary during the
                  process – for example, over a roadblock that cannot be solved right away.

 DON’T call it “role-play”! The term is
 unpopular because it is misused in many training
 courses. Technically, investigative rehearsal
 is not role-play, but it looks very similar. So, call it
 rehearsal, simulation, bodystorming,
 a service walkthrough, or don’t call it anything –
 just say “show me.”

 PAPER PROTOTYPING
 In paper prototyping, the screens of a
 digital interface are hand sketched on paper and
 presented to a user to quickly test interfaces.

  BUSINESS
 ­MODEL ­CANVAS
 The Business Model Canvas is a high-level approach
 to co-create and visualize the key components of a business model
 that allows you to iteratively test and refine various options.


                Considering business models is an                      need – for example, for investment decisions
                ­inherent part of any service design ­process.         by ­external stakeholders. Instead, such tools
                 Any ­changes of ­organizational ­structures,           can c­ omplement a business plan: p
                                                                                                          ­ rototyping
                 ­processes, ­software, ­products, services,            and testing various scenarios can help you
                  ­stakeholder r­ elationships, or ­customer groups     understand the impact of various options on the
                   affect ­different parts of a business ­model – in   ­employee and customer experience as well as
                   ­return, most ­changes of a ­business ­model         on the business. Based on a refined and tested
                    ­affect the ­employee or ­customer ­experience,     business ­model, you can then easily detail out
                     and ­therefore shouldn’t be done ­without an       an ­extensive business plan.01
                     ­accompanying ­service design process.
                                                                       With the Business Model Canvas, you can
                However, the process of writing a ­complete            ­quickly sketch out the business ­model of e­ xisting
                business plan to define a ­business ­model is a         services or products, whether ­physical or digital,
                bad match for the quick and ­iterative w   ­ orking     or prototype the ­business model of new concepts.
                style of service design. Instead, you need tools        It is intended to be used as a tool in an iterative
                to quickly visualize a ­business ­model so you
                can iteratively test and ­refine ­various ­options.    01	   [EXPERT TIP from Alexander Osterwalder] “Keep your first canvases ‘rough and dirty’
                                                                             when you use the Business Model Canvas to sketch out business models for new ideas or
                These tools should not be a ­substitute for                  ­ventures. Then immediately test your early canvases in terms of desirability (will customers
                                                                             want it?), feasibility (can I build it?), and viability (will it be profitable?). Adapt your canvases
                                                                             rapidly based on what you learn from your tests. Iterate and refine your canvas with this
                a ­classic business plan, which you ­often still              ­so-called Lean Startup approach until you have sufficient evidence that your idea can succeed.”

 SKETCHING
 Sketching refers to methods of
 visualization or representation of design ideas
 that support fast and flexible exploration.


               Sketches are flexible, quick, and i­ nexpensive                                                   hardware.02 Similarly, bodystorming and
               ­visualizations or representations. Their explo­                                                  early-stage walkthrough techniques are very
                rative nature often makes them the first step                                                    ­efficient lo-fi ways to sketch (inter)actions
                in explorative prototyping. In their most                                                         using simplified forms of ­re-enactment –
                common form, sketches are prepared                                                                or sketching with our bodies.
                ­using pen and paper by making quick and
                 ­low-­fidelity ­visualizations of an initial idea
                  or c­ oncepts ­within seconds or minutes.                                                      Step-by-step guide
                  However, you are not ­limited to these tools.                                                  PREPARATION
                  Sketches can be ­created using almost any

DESCRIPTIONS
  www.tisdd.com




10
FACILITATION
METHODS
Workshops are the key working format
of service design. How can we keep them
engaging, relevant, and productive?


     	    1	 Three-brain warm-up
     	   2	 Color-chain warm-up
     	   3	 “Yes, and …” warm-up
     	   4	 Red and green feedback




                                          www.tisdd.com
